<span id="freeTextContainer12464459779763501670">A family moves to Crickley Hall, renting the property. All I can say is they must be rich! The family dog objects to being in the house and hints from a caretaker suggest some weird happenings just might have precedent. Pretty classic stuff, but then it is pretty much a classic of the genre.<br/><br/>We learn early on that the family lost a child, a 5-year-old son who went missing, and the reason for moving was to get away from their old house on the anniversary of his disappearance where memories torment</span>
  <span id="freeText12464459779763501670" style="display:none">A family moves to Crickley Hall, renting the property. All I can say is they must be rich! The family dog objects to being in the house and hints from a caretaker suggest some weird happenings just might have precedent. Pretty classic stuff, but then it is pretty much a classic of the genre.<br /><br />We learn early on that the family lost a child, a 5-year-old son who went missing, and the reason for moving was to get away from their old house on the anniversary of his disappearance where memories torment the bereaved mother. <br /><br />The story unfolds slowly, ekeing out information about Crickley Hall and its history, but it holds attention. The ghostly happenings escalate, increasing the fear factor enough that I would have got out of the house early on! We get to deal with psychics, debunkers and locals as well as ordinary family problems, like one of the daughters having trouble with a school bully.<br /><br />I couldn't help noticing some technical issues that from a new author, would get a lot of criticism, but this is James Herbert, a well-known and very successful author whose books I've enjoyed before. These include info dumps, several digressions into character back story, and scenes near the end that drone on far too long. I think 200 pages could have been cut from this book to tighten it up. One issue near the end had be questioning physical reality. The one really jarring thing was a new character brought in late, but there is precedent for his existence and it all ties together by the end. It's not even an early book by the author, but one of his latest ones.<br /><br />Despite all that, I did get into the story and mostly enjoyed it, though the ending had me rolling my eyes and seeing why it got made into a movie (more cinematic than believable). It's worth reading once anyway, considering it's a well-known story. I might even watch the movie sometime.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12464459779763501670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_404878416" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/404878416" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/554401-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233528349p2/554401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/404878416?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/554401-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer404878416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9059311877710149543">James Herbert is held with a great deal of respect in the UK: there’s quite a few horror readers out there who were weaned on the author’s The Rats(1974) and The Fog (1975) from the 1970’s.<br/><br/>Since then (and twenty odd books later) Herbert’s current reputation is, well... odd. There’s been a few books that readers haven’t been too favourable of, and it seems that Herbert’s reputation as ‘Britain’s answer to Stephen King’ has not turned out the way many predicted it would. He sells very well, and ye</span>
  <span id="freeText9059311877710149543" style="display:none">James Herbert is held with a great deal of respect in the UK: there’s quite a few horror readers out there who were weaned on the author’s The Rats(1974) and The Fog (1975) from the 1970’s.<br /><br />Since then (and twenty odd books later) Herbert’s current reputation is, well... odd. There’s been a few books that readers haven’t been too favourable of, and it seems that Herbert’s reputation as ‘Britain’s answer to Stephen King’ has not turned out the way many predicted it would. He sells very well, and yet whereas many general readers may recognise Mr King’s name, I doubt they would know James’. <br /><br />So this is an interesting one for me. Having not really read any Herbert myself for about twenty five years, this most recent book (though a new book, his first for six years, is due imminently) shows both the strengths and weaknesses that may have led to this frustration.<br /><br />The Secret of Crickley Hall is basically a haunted house story, in the same manner as, say, Shirley Jackson’s The Legend of Hill House or Richard Matheson’s Hell House.<br /><br />The Caleigh family decide to move away from London for a while, following the disappearance of Gabriel and Eve’s five-year-old son Cam, a year ago. American husband Gabe thinks that Eve could do with a rest after her near-breakdown and the two remaining children, Loren and Cally, agree.<br /><br />The family move to Crickley Hall, left empty after a couple of years. Though a large house in a seemingly-charming rural area, people, even renters, don’t seem to want to stay long. Soon after moving in, the family notice odd things around the hall: glimpses of ‘something’ moving along corridors, the family dog’s reluctance to be inside the house, the child’s swing in the garden that seems to move without a breeze, little puddles of water as if made by children’s footprints on the stairs....<br /><br />And the point that it is so obviously a haunted house tale may be the issue. The creepiness here is highlighted from almost the first page as ‘odd some-things happen’. This happens so immediately and so clearly that my inner voice soon began to question: if things are that bad, why don’t you just leave?, but Herbert’s characters listen to none of my advice and soldier on, for the good of the children, the spouse, the family.<br /><br />Now I realise that a reader should realise just by picking up the book that it’s pretty obvious what’s going to happen. Some readers will have bought it just because it promises, no, it is expected that that will happen. <br /><br />The book however fits too many clichés. Whilst a reader can feel slightly comforted in knowing what’s going to happen next, that all the pieces are falling into place, some may feel that it is just too much ‘something we’ve read before’.<br /><br />This déjà-vu was re-emphasised when we have the arrival of Lili Peel, a local psychic, who, when brought in by Eve definitely feels presences. Reminiscent of Shirley Jackson’s Eleanor Vance, Lili picks up on the presence of children and a darker, more malignant adult who seems to be stopping them from moving on. There’s a lot of filling in of back history as we read: the children were war orphans, kept in the hall when evacuated there in 1943. They were kept under the harsh regime by a brother-and-sister pairing of Augustus and Magda Cribben, whose punishments usually involve liberal use of the cane. A flood from the underground river there seems to have drowned the children and Augustus, but as the story continues, we find out other reasons.<br /><br />It is quite nice to read something so ‘English’, though it tends to pander to the clichés rather than the reality. I did get the impression that the lead male character was an American for no other reason that it is something some readers (or target audiences!) would want – it covers a certain dynamic. And that is unfair, perhaps, but it can’t be a good thing when the inner cynic makes itself known so quickly.<br /><br />Some of this Herbert clearly recognises himself: there’s a point where the family go to the local village where the folk there fit every horror staple you can think of: ancient local handyman with knowledge, busty barmaid, colloquial barman, stoic residents... even Gabe notices it when the family visit to the Barnaby Inn, in that sort’ve “Hey, this is like a horror movie!” kind of way. (Reminder - First rule of my reading: never, NEVER self-reference – it destroys the reader’s sense of disbelief.) There’s touches of other horror here: the interior monologue of a major character is like Anthony Perkins at the end of the film Psycho, to the point where some of the dialogue is near-identical.<br /><br />The book is, at over 600 pages, too long. A shorter, tauter narrative would have kept the sense of unease going longer than it did. However, in this version, events are drawn out and recapped, mused on and deliberated, to the point where the tale moves from exposition to padding.<br /><br />To its credit, the build-up to the climax is unrelenting. Even though it’s pretty clear to the reader that what’s going to happen isn’t good, Herbert keeps piling things on, just to make sure, until the ending. And the last part of the book was a real page turner, even when you thought you know where it was going.<br /><br />Some readers will draw comfort from the fact that you get here pretty much what the reader expects. It’s done well enough and with enough skill to keep those pages turning. However, there will be others that will feel disappointed that there’s nothing here they’ve not read previously.<br /><br />A book of conflicting results ultimately, then. The book delivers what is expected: it’s a solidly entertaining novel, logically written through to a satisfactory conclusion. But, for me, that’s all it does. The ending was pretty much what was expected, and fairly easy to work out from the beginning. Many will enjoy this combination of all the elements that make a haunted house tale, but I was disappointed that it was so obvious. There are others out there that are as good, if not better – I think I would recommend Shirley Jackson, Richard Matheson and Stephen King before this one, as they leave more to remember afterwards. <br /> <br />The Secret of Crickley Hall passes the time, but disappoints at the end.<br /><br />The book has been made into a BBC serial, due for transmission later this year.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9059311877710149543" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_479526438" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/479526438" />
    <a title="Pam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3007527-pam"><img alt="Pam" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/479526438?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pam" href="/user/show/3007527-pam">Pam</a>
      </span>

          marked it as dnf




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer479526438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13393404193141367764">I try to cull my TBR list regularly. Okay, fine, regularly-ish. I'm never as ruthless with my own lists as I am with, say, books in the library that need to be weeded. If it smells like a troll died in it or the spine is lettered in white-out pen, it needs to go. Pronto. But my booklist? What if I take something off and then I forget about it and it turns out it would have been one of my favorite books ever had I read it? I'm not going to use that idiotic acronym, but yes: it's a form of the fea</span>
  <span id="freeText13393404193141367764" style="display:none">I try to cull my TBR list regularly. Okay, fine, regularly-ish. I'm never as ruthless with my own lists as I am with, say, books in the library that need to be weeded. If it smells like a troll died in it or the spine is lettered in white-out pen, it needs to go. Pronto. But my booklist? What if I take something off and then I forget about it and it turns out it would have been one of my favorite books ever had I read it? I'm not going to use that idiotic acronym, but yes: it's a form of the fear of missing out.<br><br>So, while I go through and delete books that were obvious reminders to buy it for the library, I mostly cull the list by actually checking out or buying the books and seeing if I actually want to read them. Thanks to the library, this is generally free.<br><br>Like many listmakers, crossing off the things on the top of the list is the most satisfying in a weird, brain-chemical way. James Herbert's <i>The Secret of Crickley Hall</i> has been at number 20 on my massive list for several years now. The library doesn't own it, and I try to limit my ILL requests, so I took advantage of Amazon's Cyber Monday code and ordered it.<br><br>The first problem was entirely of my own making, and that is that I thought that this was James Thurber (of <i>Many Moons</i> fame) and I thought it would be intriguing to read a scary story by Thurber. OOPS. Same first name, totally different last name. But hey, people were describing him as the "British Stephen King," so I felt that all was not lost. Plus, I love a good spooky house story. Yeah! Let's do this!<br><br>Page one: "Gabe, her American husband, kept calling it [the interstate]." Well, I suppose that depends on from whence Gabe hails in the U.S. I call it the freeway, but one state down, in Illinois, it's the tollway. Or you could call it a highway. It's only an interstate if there's an "I" in front of the number: I-90, for example, but I still call that a "freeway" because it's my personal grammar. Clearly, Eve, the Obviously Very British Wife, loves to nitpick. Page two: "because his homeland was the States, Gabe had convinced their youngest daughter, Cally, that he had once been a cowboy." <br><br><a href="http://makeagif.com/IcgLOA" title="Buckaroo on Make A Gif" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1418601690ra/12844113.gif" alt="Buckaroo on Make A Gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><div>make <a href="http://makeagif.com/" title="make a gif" rel="nofollow noopener">animated gifs</a> like this at MakeAGif</div><br><br>"The Captain seems to think you're some sort of cowboy."<br><br>Oy vey. Gabe has been living in the UK for sixteen years. I would think he'd at least pick up terminology. Like does he still call the Tube a subway? Who knows? This could have been in 475 of the pages I didn't read. <br><br>Just keep in mind that pretty much the whole book (well, I made it to page 125, so for me that was "the whole book") beats the reader over the head with the fact that Gabe is American. "The American." It's really not that big of a deal. Unless Herbert is still smarting from the whole Revolution thing over 200 years ago. <br><br>Actually, the whole hyper-focus on Gabe's nationality kind of sums up the book. It's a waste of words on something that's totally mundane and that we all have heard or read a million times before. The plot is color-by-numbers. <br><br>Family suffers tragedy.<br>Family runs away to escape awful memories of tragedy.<br>Family moves into haunted house because ... reasons.<br>Creepy things start happening.<br>Family ignores creepy things.<br>Really bad and dangerous creepy things happen.<br>Family gets stuck.<br>Enter the Medium.<br>Family must break curse on house and free the spirits with help of Medium.<br>Yay all the dead people are happeeeee!<br>It takes Herbert 600 pages to do this. I think he managed the page count just by writing sentences that repeat themselves (this happens a lot; he likes to repeat himself, like this!). I had no sympathy for or connection with any of the characters, except for maybe Chester the dog, who's the only sensible one in this whole family.<br><br>This is completely skippable (in fact, I'd advise you to run far, far away from it). If you must get your spooky-house fix, stream a silly Hollywood horror film starring C-list "actresses" and "actors." <br><br>Hopefully I can return this for a refund.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="13393404193141367764" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2024017002" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2024017002" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7202176-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324164999p2/7202176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2024017002?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/7202176-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780765367334" href="/book/show/13513651-the-secret-of-crickley-hall">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=2000s">2000s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2024017002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11669466475822570645">The classic style ghost story is both my favorite sub-genre of horror and the most frustrating to me. It is formulaic, following a set pattern (move in, weird crap, disbelief, acceptance... possibly moving). This formula is part of the delight of the tale, but also where the frustration comes from. There's a familiarity, a repetition that all great ghost stories follow, because it lingers with us and makes us feel as if maybe, just maybe, there is a grain of truth to it (after all, want someone </span>
  <span id="freeText11669466475822570645" style="display:none">The classic style ghost story is both my favorite sub-genre of horror and the most frustrating to me. It is formulaic, following a set pattern (move in, weird crap, disbelief, acceptance... possibly moving). This formula is part of the delight of the tale, but also where the frustration comes from. There's a familiarity, a repetition that all great ghost stories follow, because it lingers with us and makes us feel as if maybe, just maybe, there is a grain of truth to it (after all, want someone to believe something, keep repeating it until they do). It is hard to reinvent the ghost story without losing the charm of how the tale is told. It is so difficult to do anything new with the haunted house story, that it usually comes down to how one puts their own spin on essentially the same story. When told well (Jackson's The Haunting of Hill House or King's The Shining) it is borderline sublime. When told poorly... it is just boring. <br /><br />This novel is 633 pages, it doesn't reinvent the ghost story and sadly it is boring. It is a slow burner, which is not a bad thing if there are scares and a pay off... but the book doesn't really succeed there either. The entire book feels like a giant set up, approaching a climactic final that will really stick with you, and then just fizzles off with a shrug. I'm not one who complains about book lengths, but around page 400 or so I just wanted to shout at the book to do something interesting! Don't get me wrong, Herbert is a capable writer and I like many stylistic touches... but he's working with such unoriginal (and not in the least bit frightening) material that I couldn't help but think that I've seen it all before and done better. <br /><br />There is perhaps a decent story here, but it is so bogged down in sub-plots and detours that go nowhere that I can't help but feeling that if one cut it down by 200 (or perhaps even 300) pages, the story would flow better, build tension better and just flat out work better. As things stand, there are characters who just come and go adding little to the plot, zero character development (though plenty of backstories for everyone!), moments that frankly seem a touch out of place the farther we go <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(such as the swing that seems to torment Cally, which was as we learn one of Magda's punishments for the children... yet Magda is not one of the ghosts at the house as she is still alive)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and at least one major event that seems to be setting up for something that just kind of stops in a realistic, but unsatisfying fashion.<br /><br />Frankly, the most interesting aspect of the novel came to be the play off the differences between British English and American English. Herbert quite cleverly changes how he describes things depending on the character (torch and flashlight, cupboard and closet for a few examples). This is both in dialogue and in descriptions, and leads to some amusing conversations and a few moments where you get inside the character's head in a bit of a unique way. <br /><br />In closing: this is one of those books that really wants to be big and ambitious. It wants to tell a story that will linger and haunt you for days... instead it gets bogged down in a plethora of sub-plots and just outstays its welcome. For a much better Herbert haunted house story try Haunted and give learning Crickley Hall's secret a pass.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11669466475822570645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1336800749" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1336800749" />
    <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph"><img alt="Paul E. Morph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602970987p2/44824883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1336800749?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul E. Morph" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph">Paul E. Morph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44824883-paul-e-morph?shelf=books-read-in-2015">books-read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1336800749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11504681881808071471">The Secret Of Crickley Hall is a good, old-fashioned ghost story. To be honest, it reads like a checklist of every ghost story/haunted house cliché in the history of horror fiction... but damnit if I didn't enjoy the HELL out of it!<br/><br/>You could tear this book to shreds with a long list of all the reasons it's not really very good... and I wouldn't be able to argue with you. It's genuinely creepy and deeply disturbing, despite that, and the part of me that longs to be sitting around a campfire at ni</span>
  <span id="freeText11504681881808071471" style="display:none">The Secret Of Crickley Hall is a good, old-fashioned ghost story. To be honest, it reads like a checklist of every ghost story/haunted house cliché in the history of horror fiction... but damnit if I didn't enjoy the HELL out of it!<br /><br />You could tear this book to shreds with a long list of all the reasons it's not really very good... and I wouldn't be able to argue with you. It's genuinely creepy and deeply disturbing, despite that, and the part of me that longs to be sitting around a campfire at night with a bunch of friends telling each other scary stories loved every minute of it.<br /><br />Dispassionately, with my English Lit' very serious analysis hat on, I could only give this book a two out of five. I don't give a shit about that guy, though, so I'm going with my heart and giving it a four. <i>If ya don't like what ya see here, nobody wants to take you prisoner...</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11504681881808071471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_252103342" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/252103342" />
    <a title="Jeremy Trevathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6090515-jeremy-trevathan"><img alt="Jeremy Trevathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1313926754p2/6090515.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/252103342?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy Trevathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy Trevathan" href="/user/show/6090515-jeremy-trevathan">Jeremy Trevathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330411684" href="/book/show/397864.The_Secret_of_Crickley_Hall">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6090515-jeremy-trevathan?shelf=ghost-stories">ghost-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer252103342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer655377920532519231">This is one of James Herbert's best novels of recent years. It's a traditional spooky house ghost story that takes place over the course of a week in Devon as a family move to a rented house to recuperate from a tragedy that overwhelmed them. The first sign that something is wrong is when the dog keeps running away. It's truly scary at points and doesn't have some of the gore and violence which marked Herbert's earlier horror fiction, which probably would make it appeal to a broader readership.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_905900061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/905900061" />
    <a title="Simon  Grant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23281102-simon-grant"><img alt="Simon  Grant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392634042p2/23281102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/905900061?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon  Grant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon  Grant" href="/user/show/23281102-simon-grant">Simon  Grant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781447231035" href="/book/show/16669571-the-secret-of-crickley-hall">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer905900061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6818073971363368415">brilliant read, the best James Herbert book I've read so far, for any Stephen king fans like myself this reads like a king novel. Its a fairly hefty book but so well paced you just breeze through it, not any slow boring parts in here at all, it keeps you gripped from first page to the last.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6sDmZEoOvgcpTZyiRXlvuQ7E5Cb1G/l+n2xGnX+S/JpokBYRIbsbsVw7lMnz968h38jvEENcG27Wr53GEoN3sw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gUC8bjy0bhc9upKbFGAew2C/DqxNbGr8w9aJU09Fl24DEEwbVwHLoUjMmvCi7t5bsbMFmvsriOyKFVIIIlQcRw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

In other words, a <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8107821132430433690\" style=\"display:none\">What real reader does not yearn, somewhere in the recesses of his or her heart, for a really literate, first-class thriller--one that chills the body, but warms the soul with plot, perception, and language at once astute and vivid? In other words, a ghost story written by Jane Austen?\n\nAlas, we cannot give you Austen, but Susan Hill\'s remarkable Woman In Black comes as close as our era can provide. Set on the obligatory English moor, on an isolated causeway, the story has as its hero Arthur Kipps, an up-and-coming young solicitor who has come north from London to attend the funeral and settle the affairs of Mrs. Alice Drablow of Eel Marsh House. The routine formalities he anticipates give way to a tumble of events and secrets more sinister and terrifying than any nightmare: the rocking chair in the deserted nursery, the eerie sound of a pony and trap, a child\'s scream in the fog, and most dreadfully--and for Kipps most tragically--The Woman In Black.\n\nThe Woman In Black is both a brilliant exercise in atmosphere and controlled horror       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/80984.James_Herbert">About James Herbert</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1515111311716269909">James Herbert was Britain's number one bestselling writer (a position he held ever since publication of his first novel) and one of the world's top writers of thriller/horror fiction. <br/><br/>He was one of our greatest popular novelists, whose books are sold in thirty-three other languages, including Russian and Chinese. Widely imitated and hugely influential, his 19 novels have sold more than 42 million </span>
  <span id="freeText1515111311716269909" style="display:none">James Herbert was Britain's number one bestselling writer (a position he held ever since publication of his first novel) and one of the world's top writers of thriller/horror fiction. <br /><br />He was one of our greatest popular novelists, whose books are sold in thirty-three other languages, including Russian and Chinese. Widely imitated and hugely influential, his 19 novels have sold more than 42 million copies worldwide. <br /><br />As an author he produced some of the most powerful horror fiction of the past decade. With a skillful blend of horror and thriller fiction, he explored the shaded territories of evil, evoking a sense of brooding menace and rising tension. He relentlessly draws the reader through the story's ultimate revelation - one that will stay to chill the mind long after the book has been laid aside. His bestsellers, THE MAGIC COTTAGE, HAUNTED, SEPULCHRE, and CREED, enhanced his reputation as a writer of depth and originality. His novels THE FOG, THE DARK, and THE SURVIVOR have been hailed as classics of the genre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1515111311716269909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
